action-view skin-vector-legacy"><div id="mw-page-base" class="noprint"></div>\n<div id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t<a id="top"></a>\n\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div>\n\t<div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n\t</div>\n\t<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" >Carbon dioxide in Earth\'s atmosphere</h1>\n\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\n\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t<div id="contentSub2"></div>\n\t\t\n\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>\n\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n\t\t<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>\n\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Atmospheric constituent; greenhouse gas</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png/450px-AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png" decoding="async" width="450" height="498" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png/675px-AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png/900px-AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png 2x" data-file-width="1983" data-file-height="2193" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AIRS_Carbon_Dioxide_Vertical.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2011 carbon dioxide <a href="/wiki/Mole_fraction" title="Mole fraction">mole fraction</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>) is an important <a href="/wiki/Trace_gas" title="Trace gas">trace gas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth\'s atmosphere</a>. It is an integral part of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a>, a biogeochemical cycle in which <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> is exchanged between the Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Oceans" class="mw-redirect" title="Oceans">oceans</a>, soil, rocks and the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plants</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Photoautotroph" class="mw-redirect" title="Photoautotroph">photoautotrophs</a> use solar energy to produce carbohydrate from atmospheric carbon dioxide and water by <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. Almost all other organisms depend on carbohydrate derived from photosynthesis as their primary source of energy and carbon compounds.  <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> absorbs and emits <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> radiation at <a href="/wiki/Wavelength" title="Wavelength">wavelengths</a> of 4.26 <a href="/wiki/%CE%9Cm" class="mw-redirect" title="\xce\x9cm">\xce\xbcm</a> (2347&#160;cm<sup>\xe2\x88\x921</sup>) (asymmetric stretching <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">vibrational mode</a>) and 14.99 \xce\xbcm (666&#160;cm<sup>\xe2\x88\x921</sup>) (bending vibrational mode) and consequently is a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> that plays a significant role in influencing <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>\'s surface temperature through the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Concentrations of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> in the atmosphere were as high as 4,000 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> (ppm, on a <a href="/wiki/Molar_(unit)" class="mw-redirect" title="Molar (unit)">molar</a> basis) during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian period </a> about 500 million years ago to as low as 180 ppm during the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciation</a> of the last two million years.<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge2013_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge2013-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Reconstructed temperature records for the last 420 million years indicate that atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations peaked at ~2000 ppm during the <a href="/wiki/Devonian" title="Devonian">Devonian</a> (\xe2\x88\xbc400\xe2\x80\x89Myrs ago) period, and again in the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> (220\xe2\x80\x93200\xe2\x80\x89Myrs ago) period. Global annual mean <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration has increased by more than 45% since the start of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, from 280 ppm during the 10,000 years up to the mid-18th century<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge2013_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge2013-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> to 420 ppm as of April 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The present concentration is the highest for 14 million years.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The increase has been attributed to <a href="/wiki/Anthropogenic_greenhouse_gases" class="mw-redirect" title="Anthropogenic greenhouse gases">human activity</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Etheridge1996_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Etheridge1996-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> This increase of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> and other long-lived greenhouse gases in Earth\'s atmosphere has produced the current episode of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.  Between 30% and 40% of the <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> released by humans into the atmosphere dissolves into the oceans,<sup id="cite_ref-millero95_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-millero95-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Feely04_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Feely04-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> wherein it forms <a href="/wiki/Carbonic_acid" title="Carbonic acid">carbonic acid</a> and effects changes in the <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">oceanic pH balance</a>.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_concentration">Current concentration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Assimilation_of_OCO-2_Carbon_Dioxide_into_the_GEOS_Simulation.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This visualization shows global carbon dioxide concentrations (colored squares) in parts per million by volume (ppmv).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/220px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/330px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg/440px-Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="708" data-file-height="708" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mauna_Loa_CO2_monthly_mean_concentration.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Keeling_Curve" title="Keeling Curve">Keeling Curve</a> of atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations measured at <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Loa_Observatory" title="Mauna Loa Observatory">Mauna Loa Observatory</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Carbon dioxide concentrations have shown several cycles of variation from about 180 parts per million during the deep glaciations of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> to 280 parts per million during the interglacial periods. Following the start of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration increased to over 400 parts per million and continues to increase, causing the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> As of April&#160;2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the average monthly level of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> in Earth\'s atmosphere exceeded 413 parts per million.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The daily average concentration of atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> at <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Loa_Observatory" title="Mauna Loa Observatory">Mauna Loa Observatory</a> first exceeded 400 ppm on 10 May 2013<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NOAA_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> although this concentration had already been reached in the Arctic in June 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Each part per million by volume of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> in the atmosphere represents approximately 2.13 <a href="/wiki/Gigatonne" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigatonne">gigatonnes</a> of carbon, or 7.82 gigatonnes of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> As of 2018, <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> constitutes about 0.041% by volume of the atmosphere, (equal to 410 ppm)<sup id="cite_ref-Carbon_Trends_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carbon_Trends-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ESRLtrends_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESRLtrends-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> which corresponds to approximately 3210 gigatonnes of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>, containing approximately 875 gigatonnes of carbon. The global mean <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration is currently rising at a rate of approximately 2 ppm/year and accelerating.<sup id="cite_ref-Carbon_Trends_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carbon_Trends-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-carbon_budget_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-carbon_budget-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The current growth rate at Mauna Loa is 2.50 \xc2\xb1 0.26 ppm/year (mean \xc2\xb1 2 std dev).<sup id="cite_ref-Atmospheric_Carbon_Dioxide_Growth_Rate_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atmospheric_Carbon_Dioxide_Growth_Rate-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> As seen in the graph to the right, there is an annual fluctuation \xe2\x80\x93 the level drops by about 6 or 7 ppm (about 50&#160;Gt) from May to September during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>\'s growing season, and then goes up by about 8 or 9 ppm. The Northern Hemisphere dominates the annual cycle of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration because it has much greater land area and <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">plant biomass</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>. Concentrations reach a peak in May as the Northern Hemisphere spring greenup begins, and decline to a minimum in October, near the end of the growing season.<sup id="cite_ref-Atmospheric_Carbon_Dioxide_Growth_Rate_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atmospheric_Carbon_Dioxide_Growth_Rate-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since global warming is attributed to increasing atmospheric concentrations of greenhouse gases such as <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> and methane, scientists closely monitor atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations and their impact on the present-day biosphere.  The <i><a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Geographic (magazine)">National Geographic</a></i> wrote that the concentration of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere is this high "for the first time in 55 years of measurement\xe2\x80\x94and probably more than 3 million years of Earth history."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The current concentration may be the highest in the last 20 million years.<sup id="cite_ref-Grida_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grida-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Past_concentration">Past concentration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Past concentration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Paleoclimatology" title="Paleoclimatology">Paleoclimatology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxidation_Event" title="Great Oxidation Event">Great Oxidation Event</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg/220px-Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg/330px-Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg/440px-Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="502" data-file-height="339" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_Dioxide_800kyr.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations over the last 800,000 years</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CO2_40k.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/CO2_40k.png/220px-CO2_40k.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/CO2_40k.png/330px-CO2_40k.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/CO2_40k.png/440px-CO2_40k.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="546" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CO2_40k.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Concentration of atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> over the last 40,000 years, from the <a href="/wiki/Last_Glacial_Maximum" title="Last Glacial Maximum">Last Glacial Maximum</a> to the present day. The current rate of increase is much higher than at any point during the last <a href="/wiki/Deglaciation" title="Deglaciation">deglaciation</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Carbon dioxide concentrations have varied widely over the Earth\'s 4.54 billion year history. It is believed to have been present in Earth\'s first atmosphere, shortly after Earth\'s formation. The second atmosphere, consisting largely of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> was produced by outgassing from <a href="/wiki/Volcanism" title="Volcanism">volcanism</a>, supplemented by gases produced during the <a href="/wiki/Late_heavy_bombardment" class="mw-redirect" title="Late heavy bombardment">late heavy bombardment</a> of Earth by huge <a href="/wiki/Asteroids" class="mw-redirect" title="Asteroids">asteroids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zahnle_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zahnle-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> A major part of carbon dioxide emissions were soon dissolved in water and incorporated in carbonate sediments.\n</p><p>The production of free oxygen by <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacterial</a> photosynthesis eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Oxygenation_Event" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Oxygenation Event">oxygen catastrophe</a> that ended Earth\'s second atmosphere and brought about the Earth\'s third atmosphere (the modern atmosphere) 2.4 billion years before the present. Carbon dioxide concentrations dropped from 4,000 parts per million during the <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian period</a> about 500 million years ago to as low as 180 parts per million during the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation">Quaternary glaciation</a> of the last two million years.<sup id="cite_ref-Cambridge2013_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cambridge2013-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drivers_of_ancient-Earth_CO2_concentration">Drivers of ancient-Earth CO<sub>2</sub> concentration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Drivers of ancient-Earth CO2 concentration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">Biogeochemical cycle</a></div>\n<p>On long timescales, atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration is determined by the balance among <a href="/wiki/Geochemical_cycle" title="Geochemical cycle">geochemical processes</a> including organic carbon burial in sediments, silicate rock <a href="/wiki/Weathering" title="Weathering">weathering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_gas" title="Volcanic gas">volcanic degassing</a>. The net effect of slight imbalances in the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> over tens to hundreds of millions of years has been to reduce atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>. On a timescale of billions of years, such downward trend appears bound to continue indefinitely as occasional massive historical releases of buried carbon due to volcanism will become less frequent (as earth mantle cooling and progressive exhaustion of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_gradient" title="Geothermal gradient">internal radioactive heat</a> proceed further). The rates of these processes are extremely slow; hence they are of no relevance to the atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration over the next hundreds or thousands of years.\n</p><p>In billion-year timescales, it is <a href="/wiki/Future_of_the_Earth" class="mw-redirect" title="Future of the Earth">predicted</a> that plant, and therefore animal, life on land will die off altogether, since by that time most of the remaining carbon in the atmosphere will be sequestered underground, and natural releases of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> by radioactivity-driven tectonic activity will have continued to slow down.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="Need a scholary source, not popular science (June 2020)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The loss of plant life would also result in the eventual loss of oxygen. Some microbes are capable of photosynthesis at concentrations of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> of a few parts per million and so the last life forms would probably disappear finally due to the rising temperatures and loss of the atmosphere when the <a href="/wiki/Sun#Earth&#39;s_fate" title="Sun">sun</a> becomes a red giant some four billion years from now.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Measuring_ancient-Earth_CO2_concentration">Measuring ancient-Earth CO<sub>2</sub> concentration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Measuring ancient-Earth CO2 concentration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">Climate reconstruction proxies</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vostok_Petit_data.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/220px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/330px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Vostok_Petit_data.svg/440px-Vostok_Petit_data.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vostok_Petit_data.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Graph of CO<sub>2</sub> (green), reconstructed temperature (blue) and dust (red) from the Vostok ice core for the past 420,000 years</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg/220px-Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="91" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg/330px-Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg/440px-Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="582" data-file-height="242" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Temperature-change-and-carbon-dioxide-change-measured-from-the-EPICA-Dome-C-ice-core-in-Antarctica-v2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Correspondence between temperature and atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> during the last 800,000 years</div></div></div>\n<p>The most direct method for measuring atmospheric carbon dioxide concentrations for periods before instrumental sampling is to measure bubbles of air (<a href="/wiki/Fluid_inclusions" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluid inclusions">fluid or gas inclusions</a>) trapped in the <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> ice sheets. The most widely accepted of such studies come from a variety of Antarctic cores and indicate that atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations were about 260\xe2\x80\x93280 ppmv immediately before industrial emissions began and did not vary much from this level during the preceding 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Annum" class="mw-redirect" title="Annum">years</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The longest <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice core</a> record comes from East Antarctica, where ice has been sampled to an age of 800,000 years.<sup id="cite_ref-deep_ice_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deep_ice-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> During this time, the atmospheric carbon dioxide concentration has varied between 180 and 210 ppm during <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">ice ages</a>, increasing to 280\xe2\x80\x93300 ppm during warmer <a href="/wiki/Interglacial" title="Interglacial">interglacials</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> The beginning of human agriculture during the current <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> epoch may have been strongly connected to the atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> increase after the last ice age ended, a <a href="/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect" title="CO2 fertilization effect">fertilization effect</a> raising plant biomass growth and reducing <a href="/wiki/Stoma" title="Stoma">stomatal</a> conductance requirements for <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> intake, consequently reducing transpiration water losses and increasing water usage efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Various <a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">proxy measurements</a> have been used to attempt to determine atmospheric carbon dioxide concentrations millions of years in the past. These include <a href="/wiki/Boron" title="Boron">boron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotope</a> ratios in certain types of marine sediments, and the number of <a href="/wiki/Stomata" class="mw-redirect" title="Stomata">stomata</a> observed on fossil plant leaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne,_C._P._2006_173\xe2\x80\x93194_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne,_C._P._2006_173\xe2\x80\x93194-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Phytane" title="Phytane">Phytane</a> is a type of <a href="/wiki/Diterpenoid" class="mw-redirect" title="Diterpenoid">diterpenoid</a> <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkane</a>. It is a breakdown product of chlorophyll and is now used to estimate ancient <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> levels.<sup id="cite_ref-co2_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-co2-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Phytane gives both a continuous record of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations but it also can overlap a break in the <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> record of over 500 million years.<sup id="cite_ref-co2_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-co2-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is evidence for high <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations between 200 and 150 million years ago of over 3,000 ppm, and between 600 and 400 million years ago of over 6,000 ppm.<sup id="cite_ref-Grida_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grida-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In more recent times, atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration continued to fall after about 60 million years ago. About 34 million years ago, the time of the <a href="/wiki/Eocene%E2%80%93Oligocene_extinction_event" title="Eocene\xe2\x80\x93Oligocene extinction event">Eocene\xe2\x80\x93Oligocene extinction event</a> and when the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a> started to take its current form, <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> was about 760 ppm,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> and there is geochemical evidence that concentrations were less than 300 ppm by about 20 million years ago. Decreasing <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration, with a tipping point of 600 ppm, was the primary agent forcing Antarctic glaciation.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Low <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations may have been the stimulus that favored the evolution of <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4</a> plants, which increased greatly in abundance between 7 and 5 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne,_C._P._2006_173\xe2\x80\x93194_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne,_C._P._2006_173\xe2\x80\x93194-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Based on an analysis of fossil leaves, Wagner et al.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> argued that atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations during the last 7,000\xe2\x80\x9310,000 year period were significantly higher than 300 ppm and contained substantial variations that may be correlated to climate variations. Others have disputed such claims, suggesting they are more likely to reflect calibration problems than actual changes in <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Relevant to this dispute is the observation that Greenland ice cores often report higher and more variable <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> values than similar measurements in Antarctica. However, the groups responsible for such measurements (e.g. H.J. Smith et al.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>) believe the variations in Greenland cores result from <i>in situ</i> decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> dust found in the ice. When dust concentrations in Greenland cores are low, as they nearly always are in Antarctic cores, the researchers report good agreement between measurements of Antarctic and Greenland <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_CO2_and_the_greenhouse_effect">Atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> and the greenhouse effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Atmospheric CO2 and the greenhouse effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">Greenhouse effect</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">Radiative forcing</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Faint_young_Sun_paradox" title="Faint young Sun paradox">Faint young Sun paradox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">Global warming</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_green_house_effect.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_green_house_effect.svg/220px-The_green_house_effect.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_green_house_effect.svg/330px-The_green_house_effect.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_green_house_effect.svg/440px-The_green_house_effect.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1198" data-file-height="834" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_green_house_effect.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pictogram of the greenhouse effect</div></div></div>\n<p>Earth\'s natural <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a> makes life as we know it possible and carbon dioxide plays a significant role in providing for the relatively high temperature that the planet enjoys. The greenhouse effect is a process by which thermal radiation from a planetary atmosphere warms the planet\'s surface beyond the temperature it would have in the absence of its atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-ipccar4syr_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipccar4syr-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ipcc-AR4WG1_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc-AR4WG1-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Without the greenhouse effect, the Earth\'s temperature would be about \xe2\x88\x9218&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x920.4&#160;\xc2\xb0F)<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IPCC4_ch01_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC4_ch01-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> compared to Earth\'s actual surface temperature of approximately 14&#160;\xc2\xb0C (57.2&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Carbon dioxide is believed to have played an important effect in regulating Earth\'s temperature throughout its 4.7 billion year history. Early in the Earth\'s life, scientists have found evidence of liquid water indicating a warm world even though the Sun\'s output is believed to have only been 70% of what it is today. It has been suggested by scientists that higher carbon dioxide concentrations in the early Earth\'s atmosphere might help explain this <a href="/wiki/Faint_young_sun_paradox" class="mw-redirect" title="Faint young sun paradox">faint young sun paradox</a>. When Earth first formed, <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s atmosphere">Earth\'s atmosphere</a> may have contained more greenhouse gases and <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations may have been higher, with estimated <a href="/wiki/Partial_pressure" title="Partial pressure">partial pressure</a> as large as 1,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a> (10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a>), because there was no bacterial <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> to <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduce</a> the gas to carbon compounds and oxygen. <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a>, a very active greenhouse gas which reacts with oxygen to produce <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> and water vapor, may have been more prevalent as well, with a mixing ratio of 10<sup>\xe2\x88\x924</sup> (100 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> by volume).<sup id="cite_ref-Walker_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pavlov_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pavlov-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg/220px-Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg/330px-Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg/440px-Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="807" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radiative_forcing_1750-2011.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">Radiative forcing</a> drivers of climate change in year 2011, relative to pre-industrial (1750).</div></div></div>\n<p>Though water is responsible for most (about 36-70%) of the total greenhouse effect, the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas#Role_of_water_vapor" title="Greenhouse gas">role of water vapor</a> as a greenhouse gas depends on temperature. On Earth, carbon dioxide is the most relevant, direct anthropologically influenced greenhouse gas. Carbon dioxide is often mentioned in the context of its increased influence as a greenhouse gas since the pre-industrial (1750) era. In the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fifth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fifth Assessment Report">IPCC Fifth Assessment Report</a> the increase in CO<sub>2</sub> was estimated to be responsible for 1.82 W m<sup>\xe2\x88\x922</sup> of the 2.63 W m<sup>\xe2\x88\x922</sup> change in radiative forcing on Earth (about 70%).<sup id="cite_ref-AR5_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AR5-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The concept of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> increasing ground temperature was first published by <a href="/wiki/Svante_Arrhenius#Greenhouse_effect" title="Svante Arrhenius">Svante Arrhenius</a> in 1896.<sup id="cite_ref-Arrhenius_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arrhenius-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The increased radiative forcing due to increased CO<sub>2</sub> in the Earth\'s atmosphere is based on the physical properties of CO<sub>2</sub> and the non-saturated absorption windows where CO<sub>2</sub> absorbs outgoing long-wave energy.  The increased forcing drives further changes in <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_energy_balance" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth&#39;s energy balance">Earth\'s energy balance</a> and, over the longer term, in Earth\'s climate.<sup id="cite_ref-AR5_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AR5-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_CO2_and_the_carbon_cycle">Atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> and the carbon cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Atmospheric CO2 and the carbon cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_cycle" title="Atmospheric carbon cycle">Atmospheric carbon cycle</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_cycle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Carbon_cycle.jpg/220px-Carbon_cycle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Carbon_cycle.jpg/330px-Carbon_cycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Carbon_cycle.jpg/440px-Carbon_cycle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2600" data-file-height="2192" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carbon_cycle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This diagram of the fast carbon cycle shows the movement of carbon between land, atmosphere, and oceans in billions of metric tons of carbon per year. Yellow numbers are natural fluxes, red are human contributions, white are stored carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-nasacc_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasacc-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Atmospheric carbon dioxide plays an integral role in the Earth\'s carbon cycle whereby <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> is removed from the atmosphere by some natural processes such as <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> and deposition of carbonates, to form limestones for example, and added back to the atmosphere by other natural processes such as <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a> and the acid dissolution of carbonate deposits. There are two broad carbon cycles on Earth: the fast carbon cycle and the slow carbon cycle. The fast carbon cycle refers to movements of carbon between the environment and living things in the biosphere whereas the slow carbon cycle involves the movement of carbon between the atmosphere, oceans, soil, rocks, and volcanism. Both cycles are intrinsically interconnected and atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> facilitates the linkage.\n</p><p>Natural sources of atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> include <a href="/wiki/Volcanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Volcanic">volcanic</a> <a href="/wiki/Outgassing" title="Outgassing">outgassing</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">organic matter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wildfires" class="mw-redirect" title="Wildfires">wildfires</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Respiration_(physiology)" title="Respiration (physiology)">respiration</a> processes of living <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_organism" title="Aerobic organism">aerobic organisms</a>. Man-made sources of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> include the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> for heating, <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">power generation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transport</a>, as well as some industrial processes such as cement making. It is also produced by various <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> from <a href="/wiki/Fermentation_(biochemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fermentation (biochemistry)">fermentation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>. <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">Plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a> convert carbon dioxide to <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> by a process called photosynthesis. They gain the energy needed for this reaction from absorption of sunlight by <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophyll</a> and other pigments. Oxygen, produced as a by-product of photosynthesis, is released into the atmosphere and subsequently used for respiration by <a href="/wiki/Heterotrophic" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterotrophic">heterotrophic</a> organisms and other plants, forming a cycle with carbon.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_carbon_budget_components.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Global_carbon_budget_components.png/300px-Global_carbon_budget_components.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Global_carbon_budget_components.png/450px-Global_carbon_budget_components.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/19/Global_carbon_budget_components.png 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="555" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_carbon_budget_components.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Annual <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> flows from anthropogenic sources (left) into Earth\'s atmosphere, land, and ocean sinks (right) since year 1960. Units in equivalent gigatonnes carbon per year.<sup id="cite_ref-gcb19_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcb19-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Most sources of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> emissions are natural, and are balanced to various degrees by similar <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> sinks. For example, the decay of organic material in forests, grasslands, and other land vegetation - including the infrequent activity of forest fires - results in the release of about 400&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">gigatonnes</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> (containing 120&#160;billion tonnes carbon) every year, while <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> uptake by new growth on land nearly counteracts these releases.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>  Although much <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> in the early atmosphere of the young Earth was produced by <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanic activity</a>, modern volcanic activity releases only 130 to 230&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">megatonnes</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> each year.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This small natural geologic source is also balanced by natural sinks, in the form of chemical and biological processes which remove <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> from the atmosphere.  By contrast, as of year 2019 the extraction and burning of geologic fossil carbon by humans releases over 30&#160;gigatonnes of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> (9&#160;billion tonnes carbon) each year.<sup id="cite_ref-gcb19_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gcb19-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>   This larger disruption to the natural balance is responsible for recent growth in the atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration.<sup id="cite_ref-ESRLtrends_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESRLtrends-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NOAA_CO2annual_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA_CO2annual-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>    \n</p><p>Overall, there is a large natural flux of atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> into and out of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>, both on land and in the oceans.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> In the pre-industrial era, each of  these fluxes were in balance to such a degree that little net <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> flowed between the land and ocean reservoirs of carbon, and little change resulted in the atmospheric concentration.  From the human pre-industrial era to 1940, the terrestrial biosphere represented a net source of atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> (driven largely by land-use changes), but subsequently switched to a net sink with growing fossil carbon emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-RERLCO2_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RERLCO2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 2012, about 57% of human-emitted <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>, mostly from the burning of fossil carbon, was taken up by land and ocean sinks.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RERLCO2_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RERLCO2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The ratio of the increase in atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> to emitted <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> is known as the <i>airborne fraction</i> (Keeling et al., 1995). This ratio varies in the short-term and is typically about 45% over longer (5-year) periods.<sup id="cite_ref-RERLCO2_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RERLCO2-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Estimated carbon in global terrestrial vegetation increased from approximately 740 billion tons in 1910 to 780 billion tons in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>  By 2009, <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">acidity of the ocean surface</a> increased about 30% due to uptake of emitted fossil <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-scor-int_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scor-int-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_CO2_and_photosynthesis">Atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> and photosynthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Atmospheric CO2 and photosynthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_biological_carbon_cycle" title="Terrestrial biological carbon cycle">Terrestrial biological carbon cycle</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">Carbon fixation</a>, <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 carbon fixation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">C3 carbon fixation</a></div>\n<div id="Container" class="toccolours searchaux" style="text-align:left;padding:0 0.5em;border-style:solid;float:right;clear:right;margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em;overflow:hidden"><div id="Title" style="background-color:#77bb77;font-weight:bold;padding:0.2em;text-align:center;margin:0.6em 0 1.2em 0"><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Timeline of the evolutionary history of life">Life timeline</a></div><div id="Contains-Timeline-Scale-Note"><div style="text-align:right;position:relative;top:-1em"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r992953826">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="display:block; text-align:center; line-height: 0.85em;"><a href="/wiki/Proterozoic" title="Proterozoic"><span style="font-family:sans-serif;font-size:100%;color:#ffffff;background-color:transparent;;"><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br />P<br />r<br />o<br />t<br />e<br />r<br />o<br />z<br />o<br />i<br />c</span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:20.537em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:0.75em;left:-13.05em;width:7em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="display:block; text-align:center; line-height: 0.85em;"><a href="/wiki/Archean" title="Archean"><span style="font-family:sans-serif;font-size:100%;color:#ffffff;background-color:transparent;;"><br /><br /><br />A<br />r<br />c<br />h<br />e<br />a<br />n</span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:31.956em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:-0.15em;left:-13.05em;width:7em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="display:block; text-align:center; line-height: 0.80em;"><a href="/wiki/Hadean" title="Hadean"><span style="font-family:sans-serif;font-size:100%;color:#ffffff;background-color:transparent;;">H<br />a<br />d<br />e<br />a<br />n</span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:22.520em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:-0.55em;left:-13.7em;width:8em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline"><a href="/wiki/Mesoarchean" title="Mesoarchean"><span style="color:black;"><small>Pongola</small></span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:17.841em"><div class="annot-nudge"
