href="/wiki/Huronian_glaciation" title="Huronian glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><small>Huronian</small></span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:5.372em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:-0.45em;left:-13.7em;width:8em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;"><a href="/wiki/Cryogenian" title="Cryogenian"><span style="color:black;"><small>Cryogenian</small></span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:3.489em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:-0.6em;left:-13.7em;width:8em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;"><a href="/wiki/Andean-Saharan_glaciation" title="Andean-Saharan glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><small>Andean</small></span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:2.458em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:-0.5em;left:-13.7em;width:8em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;"><a href="/wiki/Karoo_Ice_Age" class="mw-redirect" title="Karoo Ice Age"><span style="color:black;"><small>Karoo</small></span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:0.010em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:-0.15em;left:-13.7em;width:8em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="font:bold 10px Arial;display:inline;"><a href="/wiki/Quaternary_glaciation" title="Quaternary glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><small>Quaternary</small></span></a></span></div></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto;font-size:100%;position:absolute;text-align:center;margin-top:0.000em"><div class="annot-nudge" style="font-size:100%;float:left;position:relative;text-align:left;top:-1.5em;left:-13.5em;width:8em"><div style="position:relative;width:auto;z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;vertical-align:middle;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><span style="display:inline;background-color:rgba(255,165,0,0.5);padding:0px 4px 0px 4px;"><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_glaciation" title="Timeline of glaciation"><span style="color:black;"><b>Ice Ages</b></span></a></span></div></div></div><table role="presentation" style="height:4em;position:absolute;top:14.652em;left:0em;padding:0;font-size:100%"><tbody><tr style="text-align:left;vertical-align:middle"><td style="padding:0"><span style="color:black">&#8592;</span></td><td style="padding:0"><div style="z-index:10;font-size:90%;color:black;display:block;line-height:105%;bottom:0"><a href="/wiki/Francevillian_biota" title="Francevillian biota"><small>Earliest multicell life</small></a></div></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div id="Caption" class="toccolours" style="margin:0 0.5em;border-style:none;clear:both;text-align:center;width:23em"><div style="float:left;"><small>(<a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a>)</small></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/170px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/255px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg/340px-Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="830" data-file-height="1027" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Simple_photosynthesis_overview.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photosynthesis changes sunlight into chemical energy, splits water to liberate O<sub>2</sub>, and fixes CO<sub>2</sub> into sugar.</div></div></div>\n<p>Carbon dioxide in the Earth\'s atmosphere is essential to life and to most of the planetary biosphere. Over the course of Earth\'s geologic history <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations have played a role in biological evolution. The first photosynthetic organisms probably <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> early in the <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" class="mw-redirect" title="Evolutionary history of life">evolutionary history of life</a> and most likely used <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agents</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> as sources of electrons, rather than water.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid16453059_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16453059-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Cyanobacteria appeared later, and the excess oxygen they produced contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen_catastrophe" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxygen catastrophe">oxygen catastrophe</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pmid18468984_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18468984-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> which rendered the <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_multicellularity" class="mw-redirect" title="Evolution of multicellularity">evolution of complex life</a> possible. In recent geologic times, low <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations below 600 parts per million might have been the stimulus that favored the evolution of <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4</a> plants which increased greatly in abundance between 7 and 5 million years ago over plants that use the less efficient <a href="/wiki/C3_carbon_fixation" title="C3 carbon fixation">C3</a> metabolic pathway.<sup id="cite_ref-Osborne,_C._P._2006_173\xe2\x80\x93194_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Osborne,_C._P._2006_173\xe2\x80\x93194-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> At current atmospheric pressures photosynthesis shuts down when atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations fall below 150 ppm and 200 ppm although some microbes can extract carbon from the air at much lower concentrations.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Today, the average rate of energy capture by photosynthesis globally is approximately 130&#160;<a href="/wiki/Terawatts" class="mw-redirect" title="Terawatts">terawatts</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-pmid10670014_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid10670014-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Whitmarsh_Govindjee_1999_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitmarsh_Govindjee_1999-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Steger_2005_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steger_2005-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> which is about six times larger than the current <a href="/wiki/World_energy_resources_and_consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="World energy resources and consumption">power consumption of human civilization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EIA_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIA-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Photosynthetic organisms also convert around 100\xe2\x80\x93115 billion metric tonnes of carbon into biomass per year.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid9657713_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid9657713-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ps_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ps-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Photosynthetic organisms are <a href="/wiki/Photoautotroph" class="mw-redirect" title="Photoautotroph">photoautotrophs</a>, which means that they are able to <a href="/wiki/Chemical_synthesis" title="Chemical synthesis">synthesize</a> food directly from <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> and water using energy from light. However, not all organisms that use light as a source of energy carry out photosynthesis, since <i><a href="/wiki/Photoheterotroph" title="Photoheterotroph">photoheterotrophs</a></i> use organic compounds, rather than <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>, as a source of carbon.<sup id="cite_ref-bryantfrigaard_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bryantfrigaard-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In plants, algae and cyanobacteria, photosynthesis releases oxygen. This is called <i>oxygenic photosynthesis</i>. Although there are some differences between oxygenic photosynthesis in <a href="/wiki/Plants" class="mw-redirect" title="Plants">plants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>, the overall process is quite similar in these organisms. However, there are some types of bacteria that carry out <a href="/wiki/Anoxygenic_photosynthesis" title="Anoxygenic photosynthesis">anoxygenic photosynthesis</a>, which consumes <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> but does not release oxygen.\n</p><p>Carbon dioxide is converted into sugars in a process called <a href="/wiki/Carbon_fixation" title="Carbon fixation">carbon fixation</a>. Carbon fixation is an <a href="/wiki/Endothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">redox</a> reaction, so photosynthesis needs to supply both the source of energy to drive this process and the electrons needed to convert <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> into a <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a>. This addition of the electrons is a <a href="/wiki/Reduction_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Reduction (chemistry)">reduction reaction</a>. In general outline and in effect, photosynthesis is the opposite of <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">cellular respiration</a>, in which glucose and other compounds are oxidized to produce <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> and water, and to release <a href="/wiki/Exothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a> chemical energy to drive the organism\'s <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>. However, the two processes take place through a different sequence of chemical reactions and in different cellular compartments.\n</p><p>Most organisms that utilize photosynthesis to produce oxygen use <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">visible light</a> to do so, although at least three use shortwave <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> or, more specifically, far-red radiation.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_increased_CO2_on_plants_and_crops">Effects of increased CO<sub>2</sub> on plants and crops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Effects of increased CO2 on plants and crops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect" title="CO2 fertilization effect">CO2 fertilization effect</a></div>\n<p>A 1993 review of scientific greenhouse studies found that a doubling of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration would stimulate the growth of 156 different plant species by an average of 37%. Response varied significantly by species, with some showing much greater gains and a few showing a loss. For example, a 1979 greenhouse study found that with doubled <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration the dry weight of 40-day-old cotton plants doubled, but the dry weight of 30-day-old maize plants increased by only 20%.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to greenhouse studies, field and satellite measurements attempt to understand the effect of increased <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> in more natural environments. In <a href="/wiki/Free-air_concentration_enrichment" title="Free-air concentration enrichment">free-air carbon dioxide enrichment</a> (FACE) experiments plants are grown in field plots and the <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration of the surrounding air is artificially elevated. These experiments generally use lower <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> levels than the greenhouse studies.  They show lower gains in growth than greenhouse studies, with the gains depending heavily on the species under study. A 2005 review of 12 experiments at 475\xe2\x80\x93600 ppm showed an average gain of 17% in crop yield, with <a href="/wiki/Legumes" class="mw-redirect" title="Legumes">legumes</a> typically showing a greater response than other species and <a href="/wiki/C4_carbon_fixation" title="C4 carbon fixation">C4 plants</a> generally showing less. The review also stated that the experiments have their own limitations. The studied <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> levels were lower, and most of the experiments were carried out in temperate regions.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Satellite measurements found increasing <a href="/wiki/Leaf_area_index" title="Leaf area index">leaf area index</a> for 25% to 50% of Earth\'s vegetated area over the past 35 years (i.e., a greening of the planet), providing evidence for a positive <a href="/wiki/CO2_fertilization_effect" title="CO2 fertilization effect">CO<sub>2</sub> fertilization effect</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Zhu_2016_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhu_2016-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2017 <i><a href="/wiki/Politico" title="Politico">Politico</a></i> article states that increased <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> levels may have a negative impact on the nutritional quality of various human <a href="/wiki/Crop" title="Crop">food crops</a>, by increasing the levels of <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a>, while decreasing the levels of important nutrients such as <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zinc_deficiency" title="Zinc deficiency">zinc</a>. Crops experiencing a decrease in <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a> include <a href="/wiki/Rice" title="Rice">rice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potatoes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources_(natural_sciences)" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources (natural sciences)"><span title="Material near this tag needs references to reliable scientific sources. (June 2020)">scientific citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_CO2_and_the_oceanic_carbon_cycle">Atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> and the oceanic carbon cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Atmospheric CO2 and the oceanic carbon cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_carbon_cycle" title="Oceanic carbon cycle">Oceanic carbon cycle</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Solubility_pump" title="Solubility pump">Solubility pump</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">Ocean acidification</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CO2_pump_hg.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CO2_pump_hg.svg/220px-CO2_pump_hg.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CO2_pump_hg.svg/330px-CO2_pump_hg.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/CO2_pump_hg.svg/440px-CO2_pump_hg.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="505" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CO2_pump_hg.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Air-sea exchange of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span></div></div></div>\n<p>The Earth\'s oceans contain a large amount of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> in the form of bicarbonate and carbonate ions\xe2\x80\x94much more than the amount in the atmosphere. The bicarbonate is produced in reactions between rock, water, and carbon dioxide. One example is the dissolution of calcium carbonate:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">CaCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span> + <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> + <span class="chemf nowrap">H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O</span> \xe2\x87\x8c <span class="chemf nowrap">Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 2 <span class="chemf nowrap">HCO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">\xe2\x88\x92</sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span></span></dd></dl>\n<p>Reactions like this tend to buffer changes in atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>. Since the right side of the reaction produces an acidic compound, adding <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> on the left side decreases the <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of seawater, a process which has been termed <a href="/wiki/Ocean_acidification" title="Ocean acidification">ocean acidification</a> (pH of the ocean becomes more acidic although the pH value remains in the alkaline range). Reactions between <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> and non-carbonate rocks also add bicarbonate to the seas. This can later undergo the reverse of the above reaction to form carbonate rocks, releasing half of the bicarbonate as <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>. Over hundreds of millions of years, this has produced huge quantities of carbonate rocks.\n</p><p>Ultimately, most of the <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> emitted by human activities will dissolve in the ocean;<sup id="cite_ref-arch05_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arch05-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> however, the rate at which the ocean will take it up in the future is less certain.\nEven if equilibrium is reached, including dissolution of carbonate minerals, the increased concentration of bicarbonate and decreased or unchanged concentration of carbonate ion will give rise to a higher concentration of un-ionized carbonic acid and dissolved <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>. This<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (June 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>, along with higher temperatures, would mean a higher equilibrium concentration of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> in the air.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropogenic_CO2_emissions"><span class="anchor" id="Anthropogenic_CO2_increase"></span> Anthropogenic CO<sub>2</sub> emissions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Anthropogenic CO2 emissions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">Greenhouse gas emissions</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions" title="List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions">List of countries by carbon dioxide emissions</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Radiative_forcing" title="Radiative forcing">Radiative forcing</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg/330px-M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg/495px-M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg/660px-M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1268" data-file-height="616" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:M15-162b-EarthAtmosphere-CarbonDioxide-FutureRoleInGlobalWarming-Simulation-20151109.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a> in <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> if half of anthropogenic CO<sub>2</sub> emissions are <i>not</i> absorbed.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20151112-ab_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151112-ab-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20151112b_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20151112b-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20151110_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151110-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer simulation</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>While <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> absorption and release is always happening as a result of natural processes, the recent rise in <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> levels in the atmosphere is known to be mainly due to human (anthropogenic) activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Gosh03_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gosh03-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> There are four ways human activity, especially fossil fuel burning, is known to have caused the rapid increase in atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> over the last few centuries:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Various national statistics accounting for fossil fuel consumption, combined with knowledge of how much atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> is produced per unit of fossil fuel (e.g. liter of <a href="/wiki/Gasoline#Energy_content" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>By examining the ratio of various carbon isotopes in the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-Gosh03_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gosh03-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The burning of long-buried fossil fuels releases <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> containing carbon of different isotopic ratios to those of living plants, enabling distinction between natural and human-caused contributions to <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentration.</li>\n<li>Higher atmospheric <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> concentrations in the Northern Hemisphere, where most of the world\'s population lives (and emissions originate from), compared to the southern hemisphere. This difference has increased as anthropogenic emissions have increased.<sup id="cite_ref-KeelingPiper2011_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KeelingPiper2011-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li>Atmospheric O<sub>2</sub> levels are decreasing in Earth\'s atmosphere as it reacts with the carbon in fossil fuels to form <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-BenderHo2005_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BenderHo2005-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Burning fossil fuels such as <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> is the leading cause of increased <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">anthropogenic</a> <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>; <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> is the second major cause. In 2010, 9.14 gigatonnes of carbon (GtC, equivalent to 33.5 <a href="/wiki/Gigatonne" class="mw-redirect" title="Gigatonne">gigatonnes</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> or about 4.3 ppm in Earth\'s atmosphere) were released from fossil fuels and cement production worldwide, compared to 6.15 GtC in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-Peters_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In addition, land use change contributed 0.87 GtC in 2010, compared to 1.45 GtC in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-Peters_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1997_Southeast_Asian_haze" title="1997 Southeast Asian haze">1997, human-caused Indonesian peat fires</a> were estimated to have released between 13% and 40% of the average annual global carbon emissions caused by the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In the period 1751 to 1900, about 12 GtC were released as <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> to the atmosphere from burning of fossil fuels, whereas from 1901 to 2013 the figure was about 380 GtC.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><br />\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1013635363">.mw-parser-output .sidebar{width:22em;float:right;clear:right;margin:0.5em 0 1em 1em;background:#f8f9fa;border:1px solid class="sidebar-content">\n<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title"><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><div class="hlist hlist-separated">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">Atmospheric methane</a></li>\n<li><a ratio</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">\n<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title">Other</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><div class="hlist hlist-separated">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Proxy_(climate)" title="Proxy (climate)">Climate reconstruction proxies</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Deep_Carbon_Observatory" title="Deep Carbon Observatory">Deep Carbon Observatory</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Global_Carbon_Project" title="Global Carbon Project">Global Carbon Project</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_capture_and_storage" title="Carbon capture and storage">Carbon capture and storage</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Territorialisation_of_carbon_governance" Network">Total Carbon Column Observing Network</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/C4MIP" title="C4MIP">C4MIP</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r992953826"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Carbon_cycle" title="Template:Carbon cycle"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/w/index.php?title=Template_talk:Carbon_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Template talk:Carbon cycle (page does not exist)"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Carbon_cycle&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Carbon_Observation_System" title="Integrated Carbon Observation System">Integrated Carbon Observation System</a> (ICOS) continuously releases data about <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> emissions, budget and concentration at individual observation stations.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center">\n<caption><span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> emissions<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Year\n</th>\n<th>Fossil fuels<br />and industry<br />Gt C\n</th>\n<th>Land use<br />change<br />Gt C\n</th>\n<th>Total<br />Gt C\n</th>\n<th>Total<br />Gt <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2010\n</td>\n<td>9.05\n</td>\n<td>1.38\n</td>\n<td>10.43\n</td>\n<td>38.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2011\n</td>\n<td>9.35\n</td>\n<td>1.34\n</td>\n<td>10.69\n</td>\n<td>39.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2012\n</td>\n<td>9.5\n</td>\n<td>1.47\n</td>\n<td>10.97\n</td>\n<td>40.3\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2013\n</td>\n<td>9.54\n</td>\n<td>1.52\n</td>\n<td>11.06\n</td>\n<td>40.6\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2014\n</td>\n<td>9.61\n</td>\n<td>1.66\n</td>\n<td>11.27\n</td>\n<td>41.4\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2015\n</td>\n<td>9.62\n</td>\n<td>1.7\n</td>\n<td>11.32\n</td>\n<td>41.5\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2016\n</td>\n<td>9.66\n</td>\n<td>1.54\n</td>\n<td>11.2\n</td>\n<td>41.1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2017\n</td>\n<td>9.77\n</td>\n<td>1.47\n</td>\n<td>11.24\n</td>\n<td>41.3\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2018\n</td>\n<td>9.98\n</td>\n<td>1.51\n</td>\n<td>11.49\n</td>\n<td>42.1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2019<br />(projection)\n</td>\n<td>10.0\n</td>\n<td>1.8\n</td>\n<td>11.8\n</td>\n<td>43.1\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Anthropogenic carbon emissions exceed the amount that can be taken up or balanced out by natural sinks.<sup id="cite_ref-BallantyneAlden2012_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BallantyneAlden2012-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> As a result, carbon dioxide has gradually accumulated in the atmosphere, and as of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, its concentration is almost 48% above pre-industrial levels.<sup id="cite_ref-NOAA_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Various techniques have been proposed for removing excess carbon dioxide from the atmosphere in <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_sink" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon dioxide sink">carbon dioxide sinks</a>. Currently about half of the carbon dioxide released from the <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">burning of fossil fuels</a> is not absorbed by vegetation and the oceans and remains in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Excess <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> emitted since the pre-industrial era is projected to remain in the atmosphere for centuries to millennia,<sup id="cite_ref-ArcherEby2009_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArcherEby2009-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> even after emissions stop.  Even if human carbon dioxide emissions were to completely cease, atmospheric temperatures are not expected to decrease significantly for thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center" style="margin:auto;">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:35.5px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Biosphere_CO2_Flux_23122006.gif" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Biosphere <span class="nowrap"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />2</span></span></span> flux in the northern hemisphere summer (NOAA Carbon Tracker)\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ongoing_measurements_of_atmospheric_CO2">Ongoing measurements of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ongoing measurements of atmospheric CO2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/TCCON" class="mw-redirect" title="TCCON">TCCON</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space-based_measurements_of_carbon_dioxide" title="Space-based measurements of carbon dioxide">Space-based measurements of carbon dioxide</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg/220px-Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="175" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg/330px-Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg/440px-Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg 2x" data-file-width="631" data-file-height="502" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Global_distribution_of_Carbon_Dioxide.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Carbon Dioxide observations from 2005 to 2014 showing the seasonal variations and the difference between northern and southern hemispheres</div></div></div>\n<p>The first reproducibly accurate measurements of atmospheric CO<sub>2</sub> were from flask sample measurements made by <a href="/wiki/Charles_David_Keeling" title="Charles David Keeling">Dave Keeling</a> at <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">Caltech</a> in the 1950s.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> A few years later in March 1958 the first ongoing measurements were started by Keeling at <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Loa" title="Mauna Loa">Mauna Loa</a>. Measurements at Mauna Loa have been ongoing since then. Now measurements are made at many sites globally. Additional measurement techniques are also used as well. Many measurement sites are part of larger global networks. Global network data are often made publicly available on the conditions of proper acknowledgment according to the respective data user policies.\n</p><p>There are several surface measurement (including flasks and continuous in situ) networks including <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">NOAA</a>/<a href="/wiki/Earth_System_Research_Laboratory" title="Earth System Research Laboratory">ERSL</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> WDCGG,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> and RAMCES.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> The NOAA/ESRL Baseline Observatory Network, and the <a href="/wiki/Scripps_Institution_of_Oceanography" title="Scripps Institution of Oceanography">Scripps Institution of Oceanography</a> Network<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> data are hosted at the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_Dioxide_Information_Analysis_Center" title="Carbon Dioxide Information Analysis Center">CDIAC</a> at <a href="/wiki/Oak_Ridge_National_Laboratory" title="Oak Ridge National Laboratory">ORNL</a>. The World Data Centre for Greenhouse Gases (WDCGG), part of <a href="/wiki/Global_Atmospheric_Watch" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Atmospheric Watch">GAW</a>, data are hosted by the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Meteorological_Agency" title="Japan Meteorological Agency">JMA</a>. The Reseau Atmospherique de Mesure des Composes an Effet de Serre database (RAMCES) is part of <a href="/wiki/Institute_Pierre_Simon_Laplace" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute Pierre Simon Laplace">IPSL</a>.\n</p><p>From these measurements, further products are made which integrate data from the various sources. These products also address issues such as data discontinuity and sparseness. GLOBALVIEW-CO2 is one of these products.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ongoing ground-based total column measurements began more recently. Column measurements typically refer to an averaged column amount denoted X<sub>CO2</sub>, rather than a surface only measurement. These measurements are made by the <a href="/wiki/Total_Carbon_Column_Observing_Network" title="Total Carbon Column Observing Network">TCCON</a>. These data are also hosted on the CDIAC, and made publicly available according to the data use policy.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Satellite measurements are also a recent addition to atmospheric X<sub>CO2</sub> measurements. <a href="/wiki/SCIAMACHY" title="SCIAMACHY">SCIAMACHY</a> aboard <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/ENVISAT" class="mw-redirect" title="ENVISAT">ENVISAT</a> made global column X<sub>CO2</sub> measurements from 2002 to 2012. <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_Infrared_Sounder" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric Infrared Sounder">AIRS</a> aboard NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Aqua_(satellite)" title="Aqua (satellite)">Aqua satellite</a> makes global X<sub>CO2</sub> measurements and was launched shortly after ENVISAT in 2012. More recent satellites have significantly improved the data density and precision of global measurements. Newer missions have higher spectral and spatial resolutions. <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">JAXA\'s</a> <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_Gases_Observing_Satellite" title="Greenhouse Gases Observing Satellite">GOSAT</a> was the first dedicated GHG monitoring satellite to successfully achieve orbit in 2009. NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Orbiting_Carbon_Observatory_2" title="Orbiting Carbon Observatory 2">OCO-2</a> launched in 2014 was the second. Various other satellites missions to measure atmospheric X<sub>CO2</sub> are planned.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">Carbon cycle</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Global_temperature_record" title="Global temperature record">Global temperature record</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Keeling_Curve" title="Keeling Curve">Keeling Curve</a> - a graph of the accumulation of carbon dioxide in the Earth\'s atmosphere based on measurements taken in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Carbon_dioxide_in_Earth%27s_atmosphere&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output Because the Earth is much colder than the Sun, it radiates at much longer wavelengths, primarily in the infrared part of the spectrum (see Figure 1). Much of this thermal radiation emitted by the land and ocean is absorbed by the atmosphere, including clouds, and reradiated back to Earth. This is called the greenhouse effect."<br />Stephen H. Schneider, in <i>Geosphere-biosphere Interactions and Climate,</i> Lennart O. Bengtsson and Claus U.
